Equifax Inc. Chief Executive Richard Smith told a breakfast meeting in mid-August that data fraud is a "huge opportunity" for the credit-reporting company, allowing it to sell consumers more offerings.

Mr. Smith, who goes by Rick, said young people should sign up for the company's credit-monitoring offerings, according to a "https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=lZzqUnQg-Us" at the University of Georgia's Terry College of Business. Mr. Smith was responding to an audience question about data fraud and said protecting consumer data was "a huge priority" for the company.

What the audience didn't know, and Mr. Smith didn't mention: less than three weeks earlier, Equifax had discovered a systems breach and called in Mandiant, the cyber-investigations division of FireEye Inc., to investigate. That inquiry uncovered a massive attack that exposed vital personal information of potentially 143 million Americans, something Equifax didn't "https://www.wsj.com/articles/equifax-reports-data-breach-possibly-impacting-143-million-u-s-consumers-1504819765".

Mr. Smith's remarks add to "https://blogs.wsj.com/cio/2017/09/11/questions-about-equifax-breach-pile-up/" about what Equifax knew, when it knew and whether it took sufficient steps to safeguard its data. They also underscore how focused Mr. Smith has been on getting and selling more data.

Mr. Smith and Equifax declined to comment.

Until the hack was announced, the drive for data had helped both Mr. Smith and Equifax prosper. After taking the helm of the company 12 years ago this week, Mr. Smith transformed Equifax from what he once described as a staid, slow-growing credit-reporting company into a data giant.

He bought companies with databases that contained information about consumers' employment histories, salaries and savings while also expanding internationally to places like Australia and India.

The result: by 2016, credit-reporting activities accounted for less than a third of revenues versus about 80% a decade earlier. Equifax's market value soared to nearly $18 billion, more than quadruple its value when Mr. Smith started.

And the executive became a business celebrity in Equifax's hometown of Atlanta, co-chairing the city's successful bid to host the 2019 Super Bowl and being inducted into Georgia State University's Business Hall of Fame in 2010. Mr. Smith also did well financially: Over his time as CEO, he took home around $127 million in combined total compensation, according to securities filings.

The breach upended it all. Equifax has faced heated criticism from angry consumers, politicians and its own customers. The company is under investigation by regulators even as the Federal Bureau of Investigation has faced heated criticism from angry consumers, politicians and its own customers. The company is under investigation by regulators even as the "https://www.wsj.com/articles/fbi-looking-into-equifax-data-breach-1504902745". The stock market has cut its market value by $5.5 billion, or nearly a third.

Mr. Smith, meanwhile, "https://www.wsj.com/articles/equifax-ceo-to-testify-at-senate-hearing-on-data-breach-1506017076" in coming weeks. He hasn't spoken publicly since the hack was disclosed, except for a video Equifax released at that time. In an "https://www.usatoday.com/story/opinion/2017/09/12/equifax-ceo-we-make-changes-editorials-debates/659738001/", Mr. Smith said the incident was the "most humbling moment in our 118-year history."

It also shows the gaps that often exist between when institutions discover an intrusion and when they disclose it.

Equifax decided to hold off on immediately informing the public of its breach until the company had more clarity on the number of people affected and the types of information that were compromised, according to people familiar with the matter.

Mr. Smith grew up in the Midwest. During high school summers, he built silos on farms. He graduated from Purdue University and was a varsity wrestler.

In the early 1980s, Mr. Smith landed at General Electric where he spent more than two decades moving up the ranks. Jack Welch, that company's well-known CEO, was a mentor.

People who worked with Mr. Smith describe him as being in constant motion, always focused on an objective and looking to move on.

He was the same outside the office. "He plays speed golf," said one person who knows him. "I never had a three putt with Rick. You putt once, and he gives you the second one because he's ready to go to the next hole."

When Mr. Smith came to Equifax he looked to change the company and its culture, he said in his University of Georgia remarks. "A lesson I always tell people is you have to take a risk in life," Mr. Smith said.

At the time, Equifax was focused primarily on selling credit data to lenders and "probably growing 1% or 2% organically a year," Mr. Smith said. "It wasn't a culture I wanted for the future, it was a culture of entitlement."

He added that he wanted to turn Equifax into a "global data-analytics company." To do so, Mr. Smith acquired and monetized countless pieces of data on consumers' financial lives, allowing lenders, landlords and insurance companies, among others, to make decisions ranging from extending credit to hiring job seekers to renting an apartment. Equifax got that new data in part by buying other companies.

The company's biggest acquisition, in 2016, was the $1.9 billion "https://www.wsj.com/articles/consumer-credit-agency-equifax-makes-move-on-australias-veda-1442546222", an Australian credit information and analytics company.

The push into wider data sets turned Equifax into a business that housed "$12 trillion of consumer wealth data," Mr. Smith said in an interview with his alma mater around six years ago. "Without us, you wouldn't have global commerce as you know it today."

Besides work, Mr. Smith also was involved in outside boards and committees, several of which were focused on bettering the Atlanta region. "Rick wasn't a 12-hour a day, 7-day a week guy in the office," said a person who knows him.

While Equifax grew quickly, competitors privately criticized the company's technological abilities, saying it was more focused on growing data sets. Equifax "came to the software party very late," said an industry executive.

Following the breach, questions have arisen in information-security communities about whether and when Equifax patched a software vulnerability that gave hackers "https://www.wsj.com/articles/hackers-entered-equifax-systems-in-march-1505943617". Equifax, while saying its staff "took efforts" to patch systems when the vulnerability was reported in early March, acknowledged the questions and said its review "is ongoing."

The view that Equifax lagged behind peers technologically wasn't shared by Mr. Smith. We're a "world-class state-of-the art technology" company, he said at last month's University of Georgia event.

(The following is a roundup of selected companies that have revealed securities sales in recent Form D regulatory filings. These companies are required to file a Form D notification within 15 days after the first sale of securities in an offering, which could indicate fundraising, acquisitions or other securities activity. The filings don't list the recipients of the securities being offered. However, they do list board members, so in cases where new investors have surfaced, we'll report them below.)

2080 Media Inc., Atlanta, which broadcasts high school sports to fans online, has issued $5 million of an $8.2 million round of equity and rights. The company, which is better known as PlayOn! Sports, is backed by investors including Buckhead Investment Partners, Hamilton Ventures and Imlay Investments.

Koru Careers Inc., Seattle, a predictive hiring company, has sold all but $300,000 of a $3 million equity round. In 2015, the company secured an $8 million funding round led by Maveron with participation from Battery Ventures, City Light Capital, First Round Capital and Trilogy Equity Partners.

Kyriba Corp., New York, a provider of cloud-based software tools for corporate treasury and cash management, has sold $10 million in equity. Last September, the company raised a $23 million Series D round from Bpifrance, Daher Capital and HSBC.

Nanosys Inc., Milpitas, Calif., a developer of nano crystals for energy-efficient displays, has sold about half of a $15.2 million equity round. Samsung Venture Investment is an investor in the company.

Pixel Velocity Inc., Ann Arbor, Mich., a video surveillance services provider, has sold $5.1 million of an $8.1 million equity round. A March filing showed the company issuing $1.2 million of a $2.6 million round of debt and rights. Ann Arbor Spark and Draper Triangle are investors in the company.

ProctorFree Inc., Huntersville, N.C., an automated exam proctoring services provider, has sold $2 million in equity. Real Ventures and Task Force X Capital are investors in the company.

Reactful Inc., Santa Barbara, Calif., which enables websites to listen and react to visitors in real time, has sold $650,000 of a $730,000 equity round. Inspiration Venture Partners and UpWest Labs list the company in their portfolios.

Rhumbix Inc., San Francisco, which makes applications for the construction industry, has sold all but $500,000 of a $7.4 million equity round. In 2015, the company raised a $6 million Series A round led by Greylock Partners and joined by Brick and Mortar Ventures and UJ Ventures.

Roadrunner Recycling Inc., Pittsburgh, which provides an outsourced waste management service for commercial business and building owners, has sold $10 million in equity. Adams Capital Management counts the company in its portfolio.

Schedule It LLC, Elizabethtown, Ky., a provider of scheduling services for insurance professionals, has issued nearly $400,000 through an offering of convertible notes. A March filing showed the company selling $700,000 in equity. Marshall Ventures lists the company in its portfolio.

Spring Care Inc., New Haven, Conn., which offers personalized behavioral health care, has issued $400,000 of a $3.4 million offering of debt and rights. Rough Draft Ventures lists the company in its portfolio.

Tilos Therapeutics Inc., Cambridge, Mass., which is developing new treatments for cancer, fibrosis and autoimmune disease, has sold all but $1 million of a $4.1 million equity round. Boehringer Ingelheim Venture Fund includes the company in its portfolio.

VUV Analytics Inc., Cedar Park, Texas, which produces laboratory-size vacuum ultraviolet optical spectroscopy instruments that are used by analytical chemists, has sold $2.3 million of a $4 million equity round. MARKETS & INVESTING
Toys R Us investors in play for safety; Capital markets

By Eric Platt in New York 
571 words
21 September 2017
Financial Times
FTFT
USA Ed1
18
English
Copyright 2017 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Debtor-in-possession financing brings hope of bankruptcy cushioning

Investors in Toys R Us bonds and term loans are seeking stakes in the retailer's debtor-in-possession financing, which offers the greatest protection as a messy bankruptcy process looms.

The New Jersey-based company plans to raise about $1bn of new money as part of its $3.1bn debtor-in-possession financing, according to court filings. Investors may be better positioned to safeguard their investments by participating in the loan.

Bain Capital, KKR and Vornado Realty Trust took Toys R Us private in a leveraged buyout in 2005 that left the company spending more than $400m a year to meet its debt burden while facing fierce competition from the likes of Walmart, Target and Amazon.

The debtor-in-possession financing is a type of funding used by companies in severe financial distress, typically ranking as the most senior obligation in a bankruptcy. It is also designed to help the retailer keep its 1,600 stores open as it restructures its debts after filing for bankruptcy protection on Monday.

The retailer's outstanding bonds and loans have plunged in value, highlighting how many investors expected it to stay afloat. Now owners of the bonds and loans are nursing losses, and based on current debt prices, will receive a hefty haircut on their holdings if the company emerges from Chapter 11 protection.

Bonds worth $400m that mature in 2018 briefly fell to 16 cents on the dollar this week before rebounding to trade at 28 cents yesterday. At the start of the month, the October 2018 notes were quoted just a few cents below par.

"This is a sign of where we are in the cycle," said Thomas Graff, the head of fixed income at Brown Advisory. "Folks are so desperate for interesting yield ideas. They were willing to assume that the 2018 bonds would be refinanced and you could get out at par, despite the really obvious challenges around the business."

Investors are paying close attention to a $1bn term loan, which included Angelo Gordon, Franklin Templeton, HPS Investment Partners and Marathon Asset Management, according to court documents. That loan, which is due in 2020, was quoted at 68 cents on the dollar on Tuesday, down from 78 cents earlier this month. Another term loan that matures in 2018, currently trades at 54 cents on the dollar.

Stephen Hazelton, the founder of the credit document analytics group Street Diligence, characterised the toy retailer's capital structure as "extremely complicated". Guarantees between its US and foreign operations have muddied the water of who has first claim on any eventual payments Toys R Us can make to its creditors.

The Gordian knot of inter-creditor agreements and intercompany loans has made "the actual pecking order . . . a bit of a mess," Mr Hazelton said. "The guarantee relationships, the intercompany debt outstanding, all of that is extremely complicated and will have a material effect on who gets what."

Despite the risk of owning Toys R Us obligations, which rating agencies had flagged as speculative for years, the bonds and loans had mostly traded above distress territory. News that the company was considering plans to file for bankruptcy protection earlier this month set off a "dangerous game of dominoes" that quickly squeezed the group's liquidity, chief executive David Brandon claimed.

Business
Hack Response Opens SEC to Criticism; Agency, which has pushed companies on disclose cyberattacks, provided few details of its own

By Tatyana Shumsky 
777 words
21 September 2017
21:12
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

The Securities and Exchange Commission's disclosure Wednesday of a 2016 cyberattack on its Edgar database drew criticism for both its timing and lack of detail, an awkward twist for a regulator that has been pushing public companies to both gird against attacks and to promptly disclose them.

The SEC said late Wednesday that Edgar, an electronic database of filings made by thousands of public companies, money managers and brokerage firms, was hacked last year. The SEC said it learned last month there may have been unspecified illegal trading related to the hack. The agency didn't disclose when the hack occurred, when it was detected and what information hackers accessed.

The response could undermine the agency's own efforts to push public companies to educate investors on cyberrisks and more swiftly disclose cyberbreaches to the public, said lawyers, investors and disclosure experts. 

"A federal governmental agency has a particular responsibility to be transparent," said former SEC Commissioner Luis Aguilar, who now works for a private-equity firm, Falcon Cyber Investments LLC., which invests in cybersecurity. "Particularly an agency that expects "full and fair disclosure" from publicly traded companies."

The delayed disclosure is also likely to tarnish the relationship between the regulator and the companies it oversees.

"Corporate America is going to be concerned. They have a deep-trust relationship with the SEC," said Rob Blake, vice president of product management at Certent Inc., a Pleasanton, Calif. provider of disclosure management technologies. "They're going to ask some tough questions."

SEC Chairman Jay Clayton, a Trump appointee who joined the Commission in May, brought up the severity of cyberrisk in his first major speech in July.

"Public companies have a clear obligation to disclose material information about cyberrisks and cyberevents," he said at the time. "I expect them to take this requirement seriously."

Information about the SEC hack was buried in a lengthy statement by Mr. Clayton on the agency's cybersecurity efforts and released just after 8 p.m. on Wednesday evening.

There have been at least 123 breaches disclosed to the regulator by companies that file with the SEC since 2008, according to Audit Analytics. Many more hacks are made public directly to consumers each year through media reports and direct company outreach to affected parties.

Companies must report a cyberbreach to the SEC only if they judge it to be "material," meaning it is significant enough to influence an investor's decision to buy a company's stock. The rules for disclosing a hack are stricter for broker-dealers and investment advisers than they are for public companies.

The SEC has yet to bring a case against a company that was deemed to have failed to disclose a cyberincident, but officials haven't ruled out doing so.

"Can I envision circumstances where we would bring an action? Sure," said Stephanie Avakian, the SEC's deputy director of enforcement, at a February 2016 conference. "But it would have to be a significant disclosure failure to warrant that."

An SEC spokeswoman declined to comment beyond the news release and chairman's statement.

SEC staff also have pushed companies in recent years to loop in the regulator early in the discovery of any hack and to keep regional officers apprised of the internal investigation efforts to ensure any disclosures are made in a timely fashion.

The SEC's own cyber vulnerability has been part of regulatory discussion in recent years, including multiple remarks by Mr. Aguilar, the former SEC Commissioner.

"I saw the cybersecurity issues percolating on various fronts," said Mr. Aguilar in a Thursday email to The Wall Street Journal. "It's better to take precautionary measures sooner, rather than later."

The regulator also faces a different set of cyberrisks than public companies. The SEC has no public investors who could be harmed by the delayed disclosure. But the breach of Edgar could have led to hackers making illicit gains from insider trading, the SEC said.

The Edgar hack occurred two years after Congress slashed half of the $50 million the SEC set aside for technology initiatives. The agency relies on Congress for its funding.

"The federal government is facing the same security challenges that corporations are facing in an environment where they are trying to compete for scarce security talent on a federal government budget," said Heather Sussman, co-lead of the privacy and cybersecurity practice at law firm Ropes & Gray LLP.

Ezequiel Minaya and Kimberly S. Johnson contributed to this article.

Write to Tatyana Shumsky at "mailto:tatyana.shumsky@wsj.com"

Puerto Rico Bond Prices Decline

By Heather Gillers

The Wall Street Journal

English

It could take weeks to determine the full extent of the hurricane damage to Puerto Rico, but bondholders are already anticipating losses.

Heard on the Street
Is It Fintech Or Is It Insurance?

By Anjani Trivedi 
488 words
21 September 2017
The Wall Street Journal (Asia Edition)
AWSJ
B1
English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

[Financial Analysis and Commentary]

The latest frontier in Chinese insurance: the internet. But investors are perhaps giving it too much faith.

ZhongAn Online Property & Casualty Insurance, China's first online-only insurer, is getting ready to raise as much as $1.5 billion in a Hong Kong public offering, valuing the company at a heady $11 billion. That makes it look more like the rare fintech "decacorn" from Silicon Valley, rather than a Chinese insurance company that sells drone-accident policies, among other products.

The lofty tech-like valuation is more a hat tip to ZhongAn's big backers than its own business. Chinese tech giants such as Alibaba's Ant Financial and Tencent own nearly 30% of the company and will end up with just more than 20% after it goes public. The latest entrant is Japan's SoftBank, coming in as a cornerstone investor to take up more than 36% of the offered shares at the top end of the price range. Meanwhile, Chinese insurance behemoth Ping An holds about 12%. Only a thin slice will be given out to the public.

ZhongAn brings little to the table except the novelty value of an online-only insurer. For one, almost 70% of insurance is already sold online in China. It boasts a cloud-based system that can process up to 13,000 policies a second and an artificial-intelligence-based customer service chatbot. It sells policies like a Shipping Return Policy -- its top product -- which covers the shipping cost on returns of purchases. Buyers pay a premium of 0.20 to 9.9 yuan (US$0.03 to US$1.50) per purchase.

Other products include travel insurance, along with policies for diabetes patients, cracked phone screens and damaged furniture. Last year, ZhongAn sold just more than 10 policies per customer. So it is essentially a bet on high volume in the everyday insurance-bundling business, with more than 90% of sales generated from its partner platforms -- such as Ant Financial, China's largest travel site, Ctrip, and Uber rival Didi Chuxing.

But these partnerships come at a cost: ZhongAn posted net written premium income of 886 million yuan in the first quarter, up 55% from a year earlier. But given substantial expenses including fees to its partners -- more than a third of net premiums -- it ended up posting a loss of 202 million yuan for the first quarter of this year. The company said it expects to post a big loss for the full year.

At the top of its IPO pricing range, ZhongAn would be valued at more than 4 times book value, meaning all upside seems to have been priced in. Classic property-and-casualty insurers like PICC trade closer to 1.4 times book value.

This trip to the frontier is already looking expensive.

Eric Platt in New York

Investors in Toys R Us bonds and term loans are seeking stakes in the retailer s debtor-in-possession financing, which offers the greatest protection as a messy bankruptcy process looms.

The New Jersey-based company plans to raise about $1bn of new money as part of its $3.1bn debtor-in-possession financing, according to court filings. Investors may be better positioned to safeguard their investments by participating in the loan.

Bain Capital, KKR and Vornado Realty Trust took Toys R Us private in a leveraged buyout in 2005, which left the company spending more than $400m a year meeting its debt burden while facing fierce competition from the likes of Walmart, Target and Amazon.

The debtor-in-possession financing is a type of funding used by companies in severe financial distress, typically ranking as the most senior obligation in a bankruptcy.

It is also designed to help the retailer keep its 1,600 stores open as it restructures its debts following its filing for bankruptcy protection on Monday.

The retailer s outstanding bonds and loans have plunged in value, highlighting how many investors expected the embattled vendor of toys to stay afloat.

Now owners of the bonds and loans are nursing losses, and based on current debt prices, will receive a hefty haircut on their holdings if the company emerges from Chapter 11 protection.

Bonds worth $400m that mature in 2018 briefly fell to 16 cents on the dollar earlier this week before rebounding to trade at 28 cents on Wednesday. At the start of the month, the October 2018 notes were quoted just a few cents below par.

 This is a sign of where we are in the cycle,  said Thomas Graff, the head of fixed income at Brown Advisory.  Folks are so desperate for interesting yield ideas. They were willing to assume that the 2018 bonds would be refinanced and you could get out at par, despite the really obvious challenges around the business. 

Investors are paying close attention to a $1bn term loan, which included Angelo Gordon, Franklin Templeton, HPS Investment Partners and Marathon Asset Management as investors, according to court documents.

That loan, which is due in 2020, was quoted at 68 cents on the dollar on Tuesday, down from 78 cents earlier this month. Another term loan that matures in 2018, currently trades at 54 cents on the dollar.

Stephen Hazelton, the founder of the credit document analytics group Street Diligence, characterised the toy retailer s capital structure as  extremely complicated .

Guarantees between its US and foreign operations have muddied the water of who has first claim on any eventual payments Toys R Us is able to make to its creditors.

The Gordian knot of inter-creditor agreements and intercompany loans has made  the actual pecking order 	. 	. 	. 	a bit of a mess , Mr Hazelton said.  The guarantee relationships, the intercompany debt outstanding, all of that is extremely complicated and will have a material effect on who gets what. 

The backstory

Highlights of the FT s coverage of how online shopping is disrupting the US retail sector

* FT Series: Retail s clearance sale

* FT View: Bricks and mortar over straw

* Retailers to Amazon: pick me, pick me

Despite the risk of owning Toys R Us obligations, which rating agencies had flagged as speculative for years, the bonds and loans had mostly traded above distress territory.

News that the company was considering plans to file for bankruptcy protection earlier this month set off a  dangerous game of dominoes  that quickly squeezed the group s liquidity, chief executive David Brandon claimed.

Fitch, S&P Global and Moody s have since downgraded Toys R Us to reflect the bankruptcy filing, and distressed investors are likely to spend the coming days parsing through credit documents to decide if the loans have dropped below expected recovery values.

But not all investors are convinced that a Chapter 11 bankruptcy filing is the right course for the retailer that brought the jingle  I don t want to grow up, I m a Toys R Us kid  and Geoffrey the Giraffe into homes across the US.

John Lekas, chief executive and senior portfolio manager of Leader Capital, said he believed liquidation was more prudent and that the value of the company would continue to erode if executives tried to invest further in the business.

 The [Toys R Us] structure is complicated because they have been screwing around [with] this for 10 years. It is not working and it hasn t worked for a long time,  Mr Lekas said.  I just don t understand why they are doing Chapter 11 to try and revive a dead business? Why? It s dead. Why are you putting the electric shocker on it?

Taiwan electric scooter nets $300m from Al Gore, Engie, Sumitomo and Temasek, Engie, Sumitomo and

A Taiwanese electric scooter maker has attracted $300m in funding from a basket of new investors including Al Gore s Generation Investment Management, as the company shifts focus from making vehicles to expanding its battery swapping technology.

Taipei-based Gogoro, which has sold more than 34,000 electric scooters since launching in 2015, also added Japan s Sumitomo, French electricity firm Engie and Singapore s state-owned investment fund Temasek as new investors in its series-C funding round, the company s co-founder and chief executive Horace Luke told the Financial Times.

Those companies join Panasonic, HTC chair Cher Wang and Dr Samuel Yin, who were among the investors in the prior two funding rounds, which totalled about $180m. Dr Yin, one of Taiwan s richest men, remains Gogoro s biggest shareholder. Mr Luke would not disclose the level of specific equity holdings.

Much of the new capital raised will be targeted at expanding the battery network and developing the technology in the back-end system, which uses artificial intelligence to respond to battery usage and demand as well as electricity pricing in real-time.

Mr Luke, who had a close hand in developing Microsoft s Xbox and HTC s smartphones, said that Gogoro built the first two models of its scooter  out of necessity  to prove the cost-effectiveness of making vehicles that can use its batteries and charging system.

The company now sees itself becoming more of an energy utility, offering city-wide battery storage and feeding back into the power grid if needed, rather than a scooter manufacturer.

Gogoro is already in talks with vehicle manufacturers outside of Taiwan about making  smart scooters  that can use the company s battery and  refuelling infrastructure , Mr Luke said.

Since 2015, Gogoro has installed more than 400 battery swapping stations, mostly in Taipei, but also other Taiwanese cities, and via scooter-sharing in Berlin and Paris. While growth has to-date been targeted on its home turf as well as higher-end European cities, its longer-term focus will likely be in large Asian cities, like Taipei, which face macro issues of high fuel prices, pollution, congestion, constrained or defunct public transport, and existing two-wheel vehicle density.

Gogoro s raising of $300m is significant for Taiwan s fledgling startup scene, which at times struggles to gain attention in the shadow of the Chinese market.

Yuanta Securities analyst Jeff Pu, who is based in Taipei, says despite short-term pressure on tech stocks, in the longer-term Taiwan should be an attractive area for foreign institutional investors with higher yields and cheaper valuations than China, US and South Korea.

ANALYSIS
The inflation enigma; FT BIG READ: US ECONOMY

By Sam Fleming 
2,307 words
19 September 2017
Financial Times
FTFT
London Ed1
09
English
Copyright 2017 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

With unemployment low and the economic recovery entering its ninth year, inflation has been sluggish. As the Federal Reserve meets this week, officials are flummoxed by low levels of price growth. By Sam Fleming

Ron Paul, at least, has no regrets. The former Texas congressman is one of the most prominent voices among those Americans who have long been deeply suspicious of the US central bank and its power to print money. When he ran for president in 2012, he assailed then Federal Reserve chairman Ben Bernanke for debasing the currency and risking an inflationary upsurge by pumping trillions of dollars into the financial system.

"We don't have prices in the consumer market going up like in the 1970s but we should not be surprised if that happens," says Mr Paul, who once brandished a silver coin as he lectured the former Fed chair on Capitol Hill.

Elsewhere, certainty is harder to come by. As the Fed meets in Washington tomorrow, US central bankers, and their counterparts across the world, are genuinely flummoxed by recent low inflation readings.

Despite a recovery that is now the third-longest on record, America is trapped not in a 1970s-style, double-digit inflationary upsurge, but a slow-inflation quandary. Price growth jumped in August , driven by energy and accommodation prices, yet investors still doubt inflation will be strong enough to merit more than a couple of interest-rate rises before the end of 2018.

The uncertain outlook has confounded Fed policymakers just as the central bank prepares for a leadership overhaul in the new year. President Donald Trump, who will decide shortly on the replacement for current Fed chair Janet Yellen , has managed to be on both sides of the question - slamming low rates last year during the election but welcoming them this year.

"It is a puzzle and raises a real question what the Fed should do next; I would have thought we would have been seeing more inflation pressures by now," says Jon Faust, a former adviser to Ms Yellen at the Fed now at Johns Hopkins University. "At a time when there is a confusing economic picture we don't know who will be judging that picture in a few months' time at the Fed. That added uncertainty is not a good thing."

Having lifted rates twice this year in the face of this dreary inflation, the Fed is expected to keep the target range for its key rate at 1-1.25 per cent on Wednesday, even as it announces the gradual unwinding of its quantitative easing programme . Ms Yellen is likely to leave open the prospect of a further rate rise in December, as she banks on diminishing slack in the economy driving up prices amid a global growth rebound.

Yet the Fed's 2 per cent inflation target still appears out of reach - stripping out food and energy costs, it has not attained that rate for half a decade. It has been a quarter of a century since the Fed's favoured measure of inflation - personal consumption expenditures excluding food and energy - last punched up above the still relatively sedate level of 3 per cent. It was just 1.4 per cent in the year to July. Wage growth , meanwhile, remains well below its pre-crisis pace at just 2.5 per cent.

Tools for the next downturn

In 2015 Ms Yellen set out a clear road map to higher goods and services inflation, making the traditional central bankers' argument that diminishing spare capacity would ultimately stoke up price and wage pressures. The trouble is that even as the economy grows steadily and joblessness falls to 4.4 per cent , inflation has this year undershot the levels suggested by her model.

Part of the problem is that the link between low unemployment and higher price growth embodied in the so-called Phillips curve has looked fragile for decades. Some officials suspect the Fed can afford an even stronger labour market without worrying about excessive price growth.

On one level, sluggish inflation combined with respectable growth is far from a bad thing - central bankers in the 1970s would have looked on the current paradigm with envy. But if inflation hovers too low it leaves the economy uncomfortably close to deflation and could damage the credibility of an institution that is meant to target 2 per cent inflation. Low rates and low inflation leave little rate-cutting firepower when the next downturn strikes.

Weak inflation may also prompt the central bank to leave policy so loose that it inadvertently stokes up a cycle of boom and bust - something the Fed has repeatedly been accused of doing in recent decades. That worry has reasserted itself thanks to soaring asset valuations . "The risk is that you are going to end up with interest rates that are too low relative to what is consistent with financial and macroeconomic stability," says Claudio Borio, head of the monetary and economic department at the Bank for International Settlements. "If inflation doesn't show up, it will be hard to move interest rates up at the right speed to address this issue."

A five-month-long string of weak figures this year has prompted some Fed policymakers, including Bill Dudley of the New York Fed and Rob Kaplan of the Dallas Fed, to start asking searching questions. Are globalisation and technological advances restraining inflation at a time when a cyclical pick-up might otherwise drive it higher?

"These two forces are colliding," Mr Kaplan told the Financial Times last month. "The cyclical forces we have understood historically; the structural forces are somewhat new, particularly technology-enabled disruption."

Some policymakers cite the increased ease with which shoppers can compare prices on the internet and the impact these changes have on brand loyalty and pricing power. Amazon is entering grocery retail via Whole Foods , while the hotel sector is being overturned by Airbnb. Rick Rieder, chief investment officer of fixed income at BlackRock, sees more price disruption to come, as artificial intelligence promises to further transform some industries.

Research from Adobe Analytics shows what online competition is doing in some fields. Online prices of furniture and bedding are down 8.3 per cent over the past two years, whereas the equivalent category in the consumer price index, which includes offline sales, has dropped less than half that amount. Sporting goods prices online are down 10 per cent, compared to 3.7 per cent in the CPI. Online clothing prices are down 6.5 per cent in the same period, compared with a 4.1 per cent offline drop.

Goods price deflation is not new in the US, and Pete Klenow of Stanford University says comparisons between online and offline prices baskets can be tricky because of their different constructions. While some Fed officials argue that technology is eroding companies' ability to lift prices, Goldman Sachs analysts recently found an increase in corporate pricing power over the past 20 years, consistent with a rise in profit margins and industry concentration.

Goldman economists also question arguments that ecommerce, which accounts for around 8 per cent of retail sales - is having an appreciable impact on broader inflation. The effects of online competition on traditional retailers' pricing may be no larger than those seen during the rise of "big box" superstore chains like Walmart.

Other analysts have been looking closely at the labour market, linking paltry wage growth to the soggy inflation readings. Catherine Mann, the chief economist at the OECD, the Paris-based club of mostly rich nations, says higher job insecurity and lower labour force mobility in the US may be stifling calls for higher wages. "Workers and producers are unsure that the market is actually going to be robust enough to support higher wage and price demands."

While goods price inflation has been negative for much of the post-crisis period, Ms Mann highlights a weakening in services price inflation from 3 per cent before the crisis to closer to 2 per cent now. She is examining the growing role of services jobs held disproportionately by women who are paid less than men. "It is really tough to get inflation up if wages are not rising," she says.

Official confusion

At the Fed, some officials struggle to see why big structural factors would have intervened to crush inflation numbers. Instead, the Fed has suggested this year's dull outcomes are being driven by a series of coincidental, one-off drags, including a fall in wireless data prices.

If they are right, the jump in inflation last month could herald further gains - with an extra lift potentially from the recent dollar depreciation. Yet Fed rate-setters are split over the outlook, and market confidence in Fed forecasts has ebbed. Charles Plosser, a former president of the Philadelphia Federal Reserve, says: "I do think economists need to work harder on understanding the inflation process."

The shortfalls are by no means confined to the US. In the euro area, a sharp drop in unemployment across the region is yet to be matched by a strong pick-up in wages - without which officials fear inflation will fail to hit its goal of just under 2 per cent.

Mario Draghi, president of the European Central Bank, said this year that "backward-looking" negotiation of nominal wages that reference low inflation rates may be restraining prices. Many of the new jobs are also in temporary or part-time employment, which may slow the growth of nominal wages as well. At the Bank of Japan, meanwhile, officials' struggles with achieving inflation targets are well documented.

In the US, whose monetary policy has global reach, questions about inflation are compounded by the uncertainty over who will call the shots from February. Mr Trump will have the opportunity to appoint up to five new Fed board members by next year, but attempting to predict his choices seems futile given the capricious nature of his personnel decisions.

Some analysts argue that the central bank could end up drifting in a hawkish direction - which would worry economists with a firm eye on low inflation. Mr Trump may not reappoint Ms Yellen, who inhabits the dovish end of the policy spectrum. A number of the alternative candidates for chair have shown signs of more hawkish leanings - among them the Stanford academics Kevin Warsh and John Taylor.

On the other hand, Mr Trump has called himself a " low-rates person " and there are precedents for presidents leaning on Fed leadership to keep policy loose - even when inflation threatens to start to pick up. Richard Nixon pressured Arthur Burns to keep rates low in the early 1970s, for example. While an inflationary upsurge may currently seem unlikely, economists have such a thin grasp on what drives inflation that it is far from impossible - as observers including Mr Paul continue to warn.

If inflation fails to reassert itself, the Fed and other central banks will find it harder to balance the need for low rates against the hazardous side-effects loose policy engenders in markets. Mr Faust says: "If the central bank keeps pushing it can make inflation go up - but the question is whether some other excess bubbles up first."

The low-inflation enigma looks set to continue bedevilling policy not only in the US but across the world.

Additional reporting by Claire Jones in Frankfurt

Brexit effect

Pound's depreciation feeds into rising prices

Unlike the US, the UK is witnessing a burst of inflation that is causing a dilemma for the Bank of England . But the reasons have little to do with low interest rates and quantitative easing and much more to do with Brexit .

Inflation in the UK, like that in the US and across the eurozone, had been firmly stuck below the BoE's 2 per cent target until the British public voted to leave the EU in June 2016; inflation hit a low of -0.1 per cent in September 2015. But the depreciation of the pound since the vote last year has pushed up the price of imported products, which has fed through into consumer prices. The trade-weighted sterling exchange rate has fallen by 11 per cent since the referendum and had dropped by as much as 16 per cent at its lowest point in mid-October last year.

This helped push inflation to a five-year high of 2.9 per cent in August, with many analysts predicting it will continue to move higher over the next few months and peak somewhere at or above 3 per cent towards the end of the year.

Many businesses had taken out currency hedges to cushion the impact of a fall in the value of sterling last year. But most of these hedges were for no more than 12 months, meaning the fall in the pound has gradually been felt by companies in the cost of imported goods and passed on to consumers. A pick-up in oil and other commodity prices has also boosted inflation.

But there is little sign that the depreciation-driven inflation is leading to any pick-up in domestically generated price rises.

Wage growth has remained subdued and the rate of growth of prices for services has not accelerated over the past year. And with sterling rising in recent weeks, some of the inflationary pressure from the currency could also dissipate.

Gemma Tetlow

Jobs jolt Some say the Fed can afford an even hotter labour market without worrying about excessive price growth

Latest disrupter The effects of online competition on prices may be no larger than the rise of big stores like Walmart

Perils of price rises Officials will find it hard to balance low rates against the impact of loose policy in asset markets

Heard on the Street
Markets
Nvidia's Gains Are Real, Not Virtual; Fading hype over cryptocurrency brings focus back to more real opportunities for chipmaker

By Dan Gallagher

19 September 2017
17:29
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Virtual money got real enough for Nvidia of late. But investors are right to focus on the chip maker's more tangible opportunities.

Nvidia's graphics processor chips, already widely used in gaming PCs, have also proven popular for "mining" cryptocurrencies such as ethereum. Surging prices for ethereum and bitcoin over the summer led to a jump in orders for graphic chip cards that gave a $150 million boost to Nvidia's "https://www.wsj.com/articles/nvidias-cloud-still-has-a-big-silver-lining-1502471482" ended July 30 amounting to nearly 7% of total sales for the period.

But cryptocurrency is a "https://www.wsj.com/articles/bitcoins-wild-ride-shows-the-truth-it-is-probably-worth-zero-1505760623". And recent "https://www.wsj.com/articles/china-widens-bitcoin-crackdown-beyond-commercial-trading-1505733976" to outlaw the purchase and sale of those currencies has hurt the market. Prices on ethereum and bitcoin slumped 45% and 26% respectively in the first two weeks of September, according to data from CoinDesk.

That would appear to pose at least some risk for Nvidia, particularly after the stock's record gains in the last two years and its 80% rally since early May. Nvidia now trades at more than 52 times forward earnings one of the highest multiples in semiconductors.

But most of that run-up can be credited to the company's success in artificial intelligence. Graphics chips play a vital role in this technology and are enjoying a boom in demand driven by data centers and self-driving cars. Nvidia's data center business has nearly tripled in size over the last year. Vivek Arya of Bank of America Merrill Lynch projects this segment will total nearly $4 billion in revenue by fiscal 2020 more than double its size now. When it comes to Nvidia, real money always beats the virtual kind.

The Engine, Founded by MIT, Raises $200 Million First Fund; New firm has announced its first seven investments in emerging technology

By Tomio Geron

MIT has created a new venture-capital firm to invest in emerging technologies.

The firm, called the Engine, has raised $200 million for its first fund, of which MIT has contributed $25 million.

The Cambridge, Mass.-based firm is investing in pre-seed and seed deals for ambitious undertakings in aerospace, advanced materials, biotech, genetic engineering and energy. It can also make follow-on investments in later-stage deals.

Many of the Engine's companies will be housed in the firm's 26,000 square foot space in Cambridge. The startups can get access to MIT's specialized equipment, labs and advice, said Katie Rae, chief executive and managing partner of the Engine, who previously was managing director of Techstars Boston.

"Our mission is to back and support  tough tech' companies that solve problems," Ms. Rae said. "This completely aligns with MIT's mission."

Board members of the Engine include Robert Kraft, chief executive of the Kraft Group; J. Brad Powell, managing director of investments for Emerson Collective; and Sue Siegel, chief executive of GE Ventures.

Many of its startups come out of MIT, but the firm said it would also look beyond campus for other opportunities.

So far the Engine has made seven investments such as iSee, an artificial intelligence startup for autonomous vehicles and other uses; Baseload Renewables, a low-cost energy storage company for renewable energy; and Kytopen, which aims to speed the genetic engineering of cells.

Matroid Sees $10 Million for DIY Image Detection; Machine learning  platform capable of tracking people and objects

Image detection powered by machine learning isn't just for Facebook tracking friends anymore.

Big corporate interests are now aligning around the technology that could put recognition into cameras for use in autonomous vehicles, surveillance and the myriad Internet of Things devices, offering new forms of intelligence.

One startup, Palo Alto, Calif.-based Matroid, a pioneer in computer vision as a service, has enjoyed the rise of interest in artificial intelligence applications, which has become a gateway to corporate customers seeking to benefit from image recognition.

Matroid's self-service platform allows people to set up detectors for specific images, such as a person or a car, enabling people to quickly apply those image detectors to stored or live video to identify the things being tracked. For broadcast TV, Matroid could be used to identify appearances by Donald Trump and congressional leaders in televised video, and then Slack message alerts might go out, which is what the Internet Archive's TV News Archive did in releasing its Face-O-Matic public service in July.

"We're giving computers eyes and we're essentially building a Photoshop for computer vision," said Mr. Zadeh. "We're bringing computer vision to everyone, and that means someone who doesn't even know how to program."

Business
What Comes After Passwords? A look at biometrics, behavioral monitoring and more

By Steven Norton 
1,055 words
18 September 2017
03:04
WSJ Pro Cybersecurity
RSTPROCY
English
Copyright  2017, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

What will life after passwords look like?

For many companies, the goal is for the authentication of customer and employee identities to be nearly invisible, taking only a second as a shopper logs into the website, or running in the background as office staff do their work.

To achieve that, and be able to accurately verify that employees and customers are who they say they are, companies are embracing new technologies including biometric scans of faces and fingerprints, and behavioral-monitoring systems that track such activities as what apps you open most frequently.

As hackers are getting more sophisticated, traditional passwords are starting to be seen as less secure. The recent data breach of credit-reporting agency Equifax Inc. is likely to raise further questions about using Social Security numbers and other personal data to authenticate a person's identity, and to fuel the push for better authentication methods. While security is paramount, companies are also seeking to provide a seamless experience to employees and customers who don't want to be burdened with multiple passcodes.

Mastercard Inc. is equipping some of its employees with laptops that have built-in fingerprint readers, and is testing technology that scans employees' faces before allowing them inside an office building. Exploring another advanced capability, Mastercard in March acquired NuData, whose technology can recognize customers based on how they hold their phone and other behavioral biometrics.

"The driving force is the elimination of passwords," says Ron Green, Mastercard's chief security officer.

Companies walk a thin line as they work to improve security without disrupting the user experience. Some customers may perceive their data as less secure if a password isn't required. But if behavior-based programs are too sensitive and act on a false positive, such as blocking access for a user who types differently due to a broken finger, that is cumbersome, too.

Similar challenges apply to facial recognition, even as the technology continues to improve, says Steve Wilson, who researches digital-identity issues as vice president and principal analyst at Constellation Research, a Silicon Valley-based firm that advises companies about disruptive technologies. A poorly lighted room or a day without shaving, for example, could keep a person from being able to access an application, Mr. Wilson says.

Like any authentication measure, such tools also remain susceptible to spoofing by hackers. A high-quality photo or animation, or in some cases an animated avatar, can fool some facial-recognition systems.

"It's an arms race between biometric designers and the criminals that are trying to fool the systems," Mr. Wilson says.

Companies are coming up with creative ways to combat the problem. Vasco Data Security International Inc., a maker of cybersecurity technologies, uses "liveness detection" technology which asks users to blink their eyes or turn their heads to verify that they're a living being, says David Vergara, the company's head of global product marketing.

Despite their limitations, biometric authentication measures such as facial recognition continue to become more mainstream. Indeed, Apple Inc. has said facial recognition will be used as the primary way to unlock its latest smartphone, the iPhone X, which could significantly popularize the technology.

A number of firms are turning to technology powered by machine learning to constantly authenticate people as they use an app. Such measures, which are largely behavior-based and look at a person's typical patterns of technology use, are relatively new, but corporate interest is high, says Forrester Research analyst Andras Cser.

Automatically analyzing users' behavioral patterns, which can include hundreds of disparate data points such as typing speed, is the Holy Grail for authentication, Mr. Vergara says, because it allows firms to monitor security in real time rather than relying on a one-time password.

Behavior-based authentication is focusing on consumers first, Mr. Vergara adds, because of the potential to improve the user experience, and therefore a company's bottom line.

Health insurer Aetna Inc. is rolling out behavior-based security measures for its mobile and web applications, which also will include options for biometric factors such as fingerprint swipes. The technology collects the attributes of Aetna members, such as how they move through an application or how quickly they type, and feeds that data into a risk engine. Based on data about a customer's behavior on the app and what device he or she is using, the engine can create a picture of "normal" behavior in about two weeks.

Then, if someone's actions deviate significantly from their typical behavior, the system takes note. If a customer gives her phone to a friend, for example, the app may recognize the friend as a different person and ask for another form of authentication, such as a finger swipe across the screen.

Voice is currently the hot area for startups, says Mr. Wilson. There are voice-identification systems that can learn and verify speech cadence, accent and pronunciation, and other factors to create a unique "voiceprint." Some companies are building algorithms that use voice to determine contextual clues, such as whether the speaker is under duress.

Other biometric authentication measures being developed or explored include tools that recognize handwriting, scanners that authenticate users based on their ear shape, and even ID markers obtained through a person's DNA. No matter what forms future authentication methods take, Mr. Wilson observes, "what really matters is getting a biometric that consumers can use."

Companies shouldn't rely on a single authentication tool, analysts say. Experts recommend taking a layered approach to security, one that uses a combination of biometrics, behavior monitoring and, yes, even passwords.

Mr. Norton is a reporter for CIO Journal in New York. Email "mailto:v".

pict046.png

Nobody seems to know why there s no US inflation

Sam Fleming in Washington 
2,287 words
18 September 2017
19:22
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2017 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Ron Paul, at least, has no regrets. The former Texas Congressman is one of the most prominent voices among those Americans who have long been deeply suspicious of the US central bank and its power to print money. When he ran for president in 2012, he assailed then Federal Reserve chairman Ben Bernanke for debasing the currency and risking an inflationary upsurge by pumping trillions of dollars into the financial system.

 We don t have prices in the consumer market going up like in the 1970s but we should not be surprised if that happens,  says Mr Paul, who once brandished a silver coin as he lectured the former Fed chair on Capitol Hill.

Elsewhere, certainty is harder to come by. As the Fed meets in Washington tomorrow, US central bankers, and their counterparts across the world, are genuinely flummoxed by recent low inflation readings.

Despite a recovery that is now the third-longest on record, America is trapped not in a 1970s-style, double-digit inflationary upsurge, but a slow-inflation quandary. Price growth jumped in August, driven by energy and accommodation prices, yet investors still doubt inflation will be strong enough to merit more than a couple of interest-rate rises before the end of 2018.

The uncertain outlook has confounded Fed policymakers just as the central bank prepares for a leadership overhaul in the new year. President Donald Trump, who will decide shortly on the replacement for current Fed chair Janet Yellen, has managed to be on both sides of the question   slamming low rates last year during the election but welcoming them this year.

 It is a puzzle and raises a real question what the Fed should do next; I would have thought we would have been seeing more inflation pressures by now,  says Jon Faust, a former adviser to Ms Yellen at the Fed now at Johns Hopkins University.  At a time when there is a confusing economic picture we don t know who will be judging that picture in a few months  time at the Fed. That added uncertainty is not a good thing. 

Having lifted rates twice this year in the face of this dreary inflation, the Fed is expected to keep the target range for its key rate at 1-1.25 per cent on Wednesday, even as it announces the gradual unwinding of its quantitative easing programme. Ms Yellen is likely to leave open the prospect of a further rate rise in December, as she banks on diminishing slack in the economy driving up prices amid a global growth rebound.

Yet the Fed s 2 per cent inflation target still appears out of reach   stripping out food and energy costs, it has not attained that rate for half a decade. It has been a quarter of a century since the Fed s favoured measure of inflation   personal consumption expenditures excluding food and energy   last punched up above the still relatively sedate level of 3 per cent. It was just 1.4 per cent in the year to July. Wage growth, meanwhile, remains well below its pre-crisis pace at just 2.5 per cent.

In 2015 Ms Yellen set out a clear road map to higher goods and services inflation, making the traditional central bankers  argument that diminishing spare capacity would ultimately stoke up price and wage pressures. The trouble is that even as the economy grows steadily and joblessness falls to 4.4 per cent, inflation has this year undershot the levels suggested by her model.

Part of the problem is that the link between low unemployment and higher price growth embodied in the so-called Phillips curve has looked fragile for decades. Some officials suspect the Fed can afford an even stronger labour market without worrying about excessive price growth.

On one level, sluggish inflation combined with respectable growth is far from a bad thing   central bankers in the 1970s would have looked on the current paradigm with envy. But if inflation hovers too low it leaves the economy uncomfortably close to deflation and could damage the credibility of an institution that is meant to target 2 per cent inflation. Low rates and low inflation leave little rate-cutting firepower when the next downturn strikes.

Fed chair: two hawkish contenders

Kevin Warsh

A former Fed governor currently at Stanford, he has called for reform of the central bank and has endorsed Donald Trump s goal of a 3% growth rate.

John Taylor

Also a Stanford economist who served in the George W Bush administration, he co-wrote a paper with Mr Warsh on the 3% growth target.

...

Weak inflation may also prompt the central bank to leave policy so loose that it inadvertently stokes up a cycle of boom and bust   something the Fed has repeatedly been accused of doing in recent decades. That worry has reasserted itself thanks to soaring asset valuations.  The risk is that you are going to end up with interest rates that are too low relative to what is consistent with financial and macroeconomic stability,  says Claudio Borio, head of the monetary and economic department at the Bank for International Settlements.  If inflation doesn t show up, it will be hard to move interest rates up at the right speed to address this issue. 

A five-month-long string of weak figures this year has prompted some Fed policymakers, including Bill Dudley of the New York Fed and Rob Kaplan of the Dallas Fed, to start asking searching questions. Are globalisation and technological advances restraining inflation at a time when a cyclical pick-up might otherwise drive it higher?

 These two forces are colliding,  Mr Kaplan told the Financial Times last month.  The cyclical forces we have understood historically; the structural forces are somewhat new, particularly technology-enabled disruption. 

Some policymakers cite the increased ease with which shoppers can compare prices on the internet and the impact these changes have on brand loyalty and pricing power. Amazon is entering grocery retail via Whole Foods, while the hotel sector is being overturned by Airbnb. Rick Rieder, chief investment officer of fixed income at BlackRock, sees more price disruption to come, as artificial intelligence promises to further transform some industries.

Research from Adobe Analytics shows what online competition is doing in some fields. Online prices of furniture and bedding are down 8.3 per cent over the past two years, whereas the equivalent category in the consumer price index, which includes offline sales, has dropped less than half that amount. Sporting goods prices online are down 10 per cent, compared to 3.7 per cent in the CPI. Online clothing prices are down 6.5 per cent in the same period, compared with a 4.1 per cent offline drop.

Goods price deflation is not new in the US, and Pete Klenow of Stanford University says comparisons between online and offline prices baskets can be tricky because of their different constructions. While some Fed officials argue that technology is eroding companies  ability to lift prices, Goldman Sachs analysts recently found an increase in corporate pricing power over the past 20 years, consistent with a rise in profit margins and industry concentration.

Goldman economists also question arguments that ecommerce, which accounts for around 8 per cent of retail sales   is having an appreciable impact on broader inflation. The effects of online competition on traditional retailers  pricing may be no larger than those seen during the rise of  big box  superstore chains like Walmart.

Other analysts have been looking closely at the labour market, linking paltry wage growth to the soggy inflation readings. Catherine Mann, the chief economist at the OECD, the Paris-based club of mostly rich nations, says higher job insecurity and lower labour force mobility in the US may be stifling calls for higher wages.  Workers and producers are unsure that the market is actually going to be robust enough to support higher wage and price demands. 

While goods price inflation has been negative for much of the post-crisis period, Ms Mann highlights a weakening in services price inflation from 3 per cent before the crisis to closer to 2 per cent now. She is examining the growing role of services jobs held disproportionately by women who are paid less than men.  It is really tough to get inflation up if wages are not rising,  she says.

At the Fed, some officials struggle to see why big structural factors would have intervened to crush inflation numbers. Instead, the Fed has suggested this year s dull outcomes are being driven by a series of coincidental, one-off drags, including a fall in wireless data prices.

If they are right, the jump in inflation last month could herald further gains   with an extra lift potentially from the recent dollar depreciation. Yet Fed rate-setters are split over the outlook, and market confidence in Fed forecasts has ebbed. Charles Plosser, a former president of the Philadelphia Federal Reserve, says:  I do think economists need to work harder on understanding the inflation process. 

The shortfalls are by no means confined to the US. In the euro area, a sharp drop in unemployment across the region is yet to be matched by a strong pick-up in wages   without which officials fear inflation will fail to hit its goal of just under 2 per cent.

Mario Draghi, president of the European Central Bank, said this year that  backward-looking  negotiation of nominal wages that reference low inflation rates may be restraining prices. Many of the new jobs are also in temporary or part-time employment, which may slow the growth of nominal wages as well. At the Bank of Japan, meanwhile, officials  struggles with achieving inflation targets are well documented.

In the US, whose monetary policy has global reach, questions about inflation are compounded by the uncertainty over who will call the shots from February. Mr Trump will have the opportunity to appoint up to five new Fed board members by next year, but attempting to predict his choices seems futile given the capricious nature of his personnel decisions.

Some analysts argue that the central bank could end up drifting in a hawkish direction   which would worry economists with a firm eye on low inflation. Mr Trump may not reappoint Ms Yellen, who inhabits the dovish end of the policy spectrum. A number of the alternative candidates for chair have shown signs of more hawkish leanings   among them the Stanford academics Kevin Warsh and John Taylor.

On the other hand, Mr Trump has called himself a  low-rates person  and there are precedents for presidents leaning on Fed leadership to keep policy loose   even when inflation threatens to start to pick up. Richard Nixon pressured Arthur Burns to keep rates low in the early 1970s, for example. While an inflationary upsurge may currently seem unlikely, economists have such a thin grasp on what drives inflation that it is far from impossible   as observers including Mr Paul continue to warn.

If inflation fails to reassert itself, the Fed and other central banks will find it harder to balance the need for low rates against the hazardous side-effects loose policy engenders in markets. Mr Faust says:  If the central bank keeps pushing it can make inflation go up   but the question is whether some other excess bubbles up first. 

The low-inflation enigma looks set to continue bedevilling policy not only in the US but across the world.

Additional reporting by Claire Jones in Frankfurt

Brexit effect: Pound s depreciation feeds into rising prices

Unlike the US, the UK is witnessing a burst of inflation that is causing a dilemma for the Bank of England. But the reasons have little to do with low interest rates and quantitative easing and much more to do with Brexit.

Inflation in the UK, like that in the US and across the eurozone, had been firmly stuck below the BoE s 2 per cent target until the British public voted to leave the EU in June 2016; inflation hit a low of -0.1 per cent in September 2015. But the depreciation of the pound since the vote last year has pushed up the price of imported products, which has fed through into consumer prices. The trade-weighted sterling exchange rate has fallen by 11 per cent since the referendum and had dropped by as much as 16 per cent at its lowest point in mid-October last year.

This helped push inflation to a five-year high of 2.9 per cent in August, with many analysts predicting it will continue to move higher over the next few months and peak somewhere at or above 3 per cent towards the end of the year.

Many businesses had taken out currency hedges to cushion the impact of a fall in the value of sterling last year. But most of these hedges were for no more than 12 months, meaning the fall in the pound has gradually been felt by companies in the cost of imported goods and passed on to consumers. A pick-up in oil and other commodity prices has also boosted inflation.

But there is little sign that the depreciation-driven inflation is leading to any pick-up in domestically generated price rises.

Wage growth has remained subdued and the rate of growth of prices for services has not accelerated over the past year. And with sterling rising in recent weeks, some of the inflationary pressure from the currency could also dissipate.

Gemma Tetlow

Letter in response to this article:

Too much money chasing too few goods / From Peter Rawlings, Chalfont St Peter, Bucks, UK

FT REPORT - FUND MANAGEMENT
S&P Dow Jones: 'Those pennies from ETF fees add up'; Alex Matturri

By Chris Flood 
1,181 words
18 September 2017
Financial Times
FTFT
Surveys FNM1
04
English
Copyright 2017 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Index provider dismisses self-indexing and is staying in London despite Brexit

Tad Hunter topped the US music charts with " Young Love " when the S&P 500 index was launched on March 4 1957. That tune faded into obscurity over the following six decades while the S&P 500 developed into the primary US stock market index and the world's most widely followed financial benchmark.

Around $8.7tn in assets are now benchmarked against the S&P 500 via index-tracking mutual funds, derivatives and structured products.

"The S&P 500 trades in every sphere. This provides a model that other countries, such as Brazil, South Korea and India, can follow to build thriving capital markets and promote economic growth," says Alex Matturri, the chief executive of S&P Dow Jones Indices.

The business led by the New Jersey-born Mr Matturri is surfing a wave of new business flooding into low-cost index-tracking funds from investors who have become disenchanted with the high fees and poor performance of traditional actively managed products that aim to beat the market.

S&P Dow Jones Indices is the largest provider of benchmarks to the exchange traded fund industry, which is on course for a fourth consecutive year of record-breaking growth in 2017. ETF assets tied to S&P Dow Jones Indices passed the $1tn milestone this year and account for the fastest growth within the division's revenues. Although ETFs carry low fees, they can be highly profitable. S&P Dow Jones Indices earned operating profits of $412m in 2016 from revenues of $639m. This translates into a profit margin of 64 per cent, far higher than most asset managers.

"Indexing is a scale business, similar to other intellectual property businesses. Those pennies from ETF fees add up," says the 58-year-old.

ETF managers, however, are fighting a cut-throat price war to win new business and are desperate to save money. Some, including BlackRock, the world's largest fund manager, are threatening to "self-index" by creating in-house indices that could replace the benchmarks offered by commercial providers.

Mr Matturri believes self-indexing is a misleading solution, as asset managers would never use a rival's benchmark to create an ETF. This could constrain growth across the ETF industry instead of fostering the competition that has driven down fees and attracted investor inflows.

"Index licensing fees are really only a small part of the overall cost of an ETF. There are 90 different index providers, so there are plenty of choices if anyone thinks our fees are too high. But some index brands really can't be substituted," says the father of two grown-up children.

He is also adamant that self-indexing would create unmanageable conflicts of interest for asset managers, which would be involved in the trading and pricing of the assets held by ETFs that they sell to investors.

"The best way to avoid conflicts of interest is to ensure that the indexing business is not part of the same shop, and not just separated by invisible Chinese walls or located on a different floor in the same building. Our only goal is to make sure that an index does what it is supposed to do," he says, adding that S&P Dow Jones Indices does not sell investment products.

The largest indexing providers, S&P Dow Jones Indices and its two main rivals, MSCI and FTSE Russell, were largely unregulated before the Libor scandal forced regulators to re-examine the critical role benchmarks play in financial markets.

Rules that will introduce a common framework and consistent approach to benchmark regulation across the EU are due to come into force at the start of 2018.

"The major index providers will be ready, but the lack of knowledge among some of the smaller players of the impact of the European regulations is quite astonishing. There could be some rude awakenings," says Mr Matturri.

The UK's decision to leave the EU has led to some head-scratching as the European headquarters of S&P Dow Jones Indices is located in London and pre-Brexit the UK's Financial Conduct Authority would most likely have acted as its main regulator outside the US. So a European regulator must be found to ensure S&P Dow Jones Indices can continue to passport its indices across Europe.

"We are currently in discussions with a number of regulators to determine which functions need to be run locally. We are not planning on moving any of the commercial functions from London , but a few compliance and operational staff will have to be located in Europe," says Mr Matturri.

A keen student of military history, Mr Matturri has made no secret of his strategic ambition to expand his company's presence in fixed-income markets. This led to the creation in 2015 of an index based on the bonds issued by companies in the S&P 500. Ivy Investments, an arm of Wadell & Reed, the Kansas-based asset manager has launched a mutual fund based on this benchmark.

Mr Matturri hopes that futures and options trading based on the S&P 500 bond index will, in time, be developed to match the huge volume of derivatives dealing linked to the parent S&P 500 equity index.

A plan to acquire the bond indexing business of Barclays was thwarted by the technical and legal challenges of extracting those operations cleanly from the UK bank, but other deals have followed. Barclays' indexing business was acquired by Bloomberg for about 520m.

Last year, S&P Dow Jones Indices bought Trucost, a provider of environmental data and risk analytics. The deal reflects investor appetite for a better understanding of the risks that climate change poses to investors' portfolios.

Determined to ensure that investors retain as much choice as possible, Mr Matturri says the company plans to further expand its range of ESG offerings across its family of indices.

"Trucost has helped to beefed up our ESG capabilities. The skillset required of companies managing indices has become more complex as markets have evolved. That is likely to continue over the next 60 years," says Mr Matturri.

Nobody seems to know why there s no US inflation

Sam Fleming in Washington
