Michaels's Take: Slow Going So Far at Clayton's SEC

Equifax Faces Bumpy Legal Terrain as Consumer Suits Mount

Florida Is Short on Insurance Adjusters, and That Could Stall Recovery Efforts

There's Never Been a Hotter Time to Freeze Your Credit

Canadian Exchange Weighs Risks in Trading U.S. Marijuana Stocks

Slow Going So Far at Clayton's SEC

When Jay Clayton became Securities and Exchange Commission chairman in May, the job came with the luxury of picking the agency's focus, something none of his recent predecessors had.

Speaking in July, Mr. Clayton said he had "inherited an agency with considerably more discretion over its agenda." That's because there are now fewer regulations to finish under the 2010 Dodd-Frank Act, thanks to the work of SEC chiefs who preceded him. The SEC might even be able to "https://www.wsj.com/articles/regulators-drop-pursuit-of-banker-ceo-pay-restrictions-1500590543", reflecting White House and congressional antipathy for the law.

So it's a bit surprising that his SEC is moving slowly so far. Mr. Clayton was sworn in May 4. It's been 133 days since then, and the commission has yet to vote on a proposed or final regulation. By comparison, the commission took 21 days to approve its first proposed rule after Mary Jo White, Mr. Clayton's immediate predecessor, took over in 2013.

Going back to the start of the 21st century, only former Chairman Christopher Cox took as many as 50 days to obtain a commission vote on his first proposed or final rule. Like Mr. Clayton, Mr. Cox was appointed by a Republican president who favored less regulation of financial markets. But under two previous Republican SEC chairmen, William Donaldson and Harvey Pitt, the SEC issued a proposed or final rule within a month of taking over.

It's fair to observe that Mr. Clayton isn't rushing into new rules because he wants to make it easier, not more cumbersome, for companies to raise capital. He also doesn't seem to face any pressing demand by Republican congressional leaders or the White House to move faster. But under the laws that govern how regulatory agencies function, loosening requirements usually requires a commission vote. Even deregulation takes time.

An SEC spokesman declined to comment.

Mr. Clayton has also moved slowly to fill out the commission's senior staff. He still hasn't named permanent directors for two divisions that Wall Street watches closely Trading and Markets, which oversees exchanges, and the Office of Compliance, Inspections and Examinations, which inspects regulated financial firms. His top choice to run Trading and Markets "https://www.wsj.com/articles/itg-executive-jamie-selway-withdraws-as-candidate-for-top-regulatory-role-1504881936" after he had been under review for several months.

The SEC chief could be waiting for two new commissioners to join before he gets started in earnest with regulatory changes. The White House has picked "https://www.wsj.com/articles/white-house-to-nominate-hester-peirce-as-republican-sec-commissioner-1500417225" and "https://www.wsj.com/articles/white-house-set-to-tap-columbia-law-professor-for-sec-on-friday-1504225067" to fill the vacancies. While both Ms. Peirce and Mr. Jackson are likely to be confirmed, the Senate could take weeks, if not longer, to do so.

Mr. Clayton has proceeded cautiously to build and execute an agenda of his own. But the clock is ticking: If a Democrat is elected president in 2020, the SEC's chief is likely to step down. The agency also tends to do less around the time of congressional elections, which are coming up next year. At some point soon, key stakeholders will want to see action on what Mr. Clayton has primed them to expect.

Equifax Faces Bumpy Legal Terrain as Consumer Suits Mount

"https://www.wsj.com/articles/equifax-faces-bumpy-legal-terrain-as-consumer-suits-mount-1505342810", which last week suffered a massive data breach, could face a tougher challenge in court than other corporate giants that have dealt with the legal fallout of data breaches, according to legal experts.

Unlike earlier retailer hacks, such as those suffered by Home Depot Inc. and Target Corp., the Equifax breach is bigger in scope, potentially compromising the personal information including Social Security and driver's license numbers of roughly 143 million U.S. consumers.

"https://www.wsj.com/articles/florida-is-short-on-insurance-adjusters-and-that-could-stall-recovery-efforts-1505381401" in many necessities. There's Never Been a Hotter Time to Freeze Your Credit

"https://www.wsj.com/articles/theres-never-been-a-hotter-time-to-freeze-your-credit-1505321727", many consumers didn't know what a credit freeze was. Now, the credit-reporting company's massive data breach has people reaching for the tool, a move that could have a host of implications for both consumers and the financial industry.

A freeze typically prevents lenders from accessing a potential borrower's credit report, although it doesn't affect existing credit arrangements such as outstanding loans or cards. A freeze makes it unlikely a firm would immediately extend new financing, which, while helping prevent fraud could also slow legitimate business.

"https://www.wsj.com/articles/ive-frozen-my-credit-for-10-years-its-a-hassle-but-worth-it-1505330595"

WSJ Pro Short: SEC Chairman Open to Softening Auditor Rule

SEC Chairman Jay Clayton suggests he's at least open to softening a long-contested rule that requires companies to have auditors evaluate their internal controls, the procedures they use to prevent financial errors or fraud. Supporters of the rule, part of the Sarbanes-Oxley Act, say it helps ensure companies are giving accurate numbers to investors. But business groups say the rule is too costly and burdensome for small companies, and the proposed Financial Choice Act pending in Congress would exempt many more companies from having to follow it. At a meeting of an SEC advisory panel that discussed the issue, Clayton says, "To get to a good audit you have to have good controls, but the devil is in the details, and we should be exploring them."

WSJ Pro: SEC Chairman Suggests the Midwest Could Use a Few More VCs

"https://www.wsj.com/articles/sec-chairman-suggests-the-midwest-could-use-a-few-more-vcs-1505339087": the Securities and Exchange Commission wants to know why you don't have an Indianapolis branch office.

Jay Clayton, the SEC's chairman appointed by the Trump administration, asked on Wednesday why the Midwest has so few venture capitalists even though the region hosts so many large, established companies. "Over 100 Fortune 500 companies but only 4% of U.S. venture funds," Mr. Clayton said at a meeting of the SEC's small business advisory committee. "It doesn't make sense you would have that robust an economy and so few people looking toward the future."

Foreign Investors Snap Up Chinese Bank Debt

"https://www.wsj.com/articles/foreign-investors-snap-up-chinese-bank-debt-1505280977" their holdings of a popular short-term debt instrument issued by Chinese banks reflecting both the attraction of a rising yuan and a growing risk appetite.

That made them among the leading buyers of "negotiable certificates of deposit" or NCDs in August, a rare position for foreign investors in any Chinese debt market. Central banks and sovereign-wealth funds were big buyers, according to a Shanghai-based banker at a global bank that trades on behalf of such foreign investors.

NCDs effectively high-yielding bonds, generally with maturities of a month to a year have become extremely popular with Chinese banks, especially smaller lenders, since their launch in late 2013. But as concerns have grown that banks are issuing them as a tool for leveraged investment rather than to meet genuine refinancing needs, they have drawn heightened scrutiny from Chinese regulators, who recently imposed a partial ban.

Bank-Risk Measures Overcompensate for Calm Markets

Bank risk as measured by "value at risk" has declined sharply since 2009, but that gauge may belie the actual risk in the market, Mark Whitehouse "https://www.bloomberg.com/view/articles/2017-09-11/how-much-have-banks-really-cut-their-risks". Value at risk measures how much a firm could lose on a given day based on the recent past, so calmer markets will show up as less risk even if a bank's assets haven't changed, he says. Adjusted to strip out the impact of the broader market, trading operations at the six largest U.S. banks are only about 25% less risky than they were in 2009, and have actually become a bit riskier over the past year, Mr. Whitehouse says. Lending is inherently risky, he says, but banks "don't have enough equity to absorb the inevitable losses from bad bets, and they're still taking plenty of risk in areas such as the trading book where they should be safer."

Stanley Fischer's Departure Reveals Weakness at the Fed

With Stanley Fischer set to resign from the Federal Reserve Board of Governors, Adam S. Posen reflects that the Fed board has "too much discretion in how any regulations and supervisory rules are applied." "https://piie.com/commentary/op-eds/stanley-fischers-departure-reveals-weakness-fed", Mr. Posen, president of the Peterson Institute for International Economics, argues that "there is a legitimate concern" that presidents of the New York Fed are "subject to cognitive capture by the leading financial firms." He adds that "macroprudential policy, and financial supervision in general, should be more rules-based and allow less discretion to central bankers."

The threat of a cyber attack by hackers or rogue nation states with access to quantum computers is becoming real enough that scientists and public officials are convening in London this week in part to "https://blogs.wsj.com/cio/2017/09/13/nascent-quantum-computing-poses-threat-to-cybersecurity/".

China is laying the groundwork for its "https://www.wsj.com/articles/china-prepares-sale-of-2-billion-in-u-s-dollar-bonds-1505292305" in more than a decade, a move toward expanding its ties with global investors as its economy improves.

Bitcoin "https://blogs.wsj.com/moneybeat/2017/09/13/red-hot-bitcoin-down-for-sixth-straight-day-looking-suddenly-bearish/", for a sixth straight day, as the sector continues to work out the ramifications of a Chinese ban on domestic exchanges and its dizzying 2017 gains come under fresh scrutiny.

WORLD NEWS
Fed policy expected to stay on course despite storms; Strategy: US growth

By Sam Fleming in Washington and Lauren Leatherby in New York 
766 words
13 September 2017
Financial Times
FTFT
London Ed1
06
English
Copyright 2017 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Harvey and Irma not likely to divert central bank from tightening, economists predict

While the physical damage from storms as ferocious as Harvey and Irma can last for months and years, the overall economic damage is likely to be fleeting and should do little to alter the Federal Reserve's broader strategy for tightening monetary policy, economists predict.

The US central bank lifted short-term interest rates in 2005, just a month after Hurricane Katrina battered the Gulf of Mexico - with policymakers at the time describing the economic ramifications as significant but "essentially temporary". Chair Janet Yellen may take a similar view in the wake of the twin storms that hit Texas and Florida.

Estimates from Moody's Analytics put the total physical damage from Harvey and Irma at $150bn-$200bn - comparable to that following Katrina. The hit to annualised gross domestic product growth in the third quarter may be 0.5 percentage points, it said. Separate estimates from Goldman Sachs suggest the hurricane-related disruptions will shave as much as 0.8 percentage points from growth during that period, lowering the likely expansion to 2 per cent.

Conversely, headline inflation could briefly spike in the aftermath of the storms, driven by factors such as higher petrol prices following refinery shutdowns, as well as an uplift in used car prices after the storms left a trail of ruined vehicles. The impact on core inflation - which strips out food and fuel prices and is closely watched by the Fed - may be more muted.

Analysts stress that big disasters can lift growth further out as the rebuilding effort gets under way, leaving the overall economy on largely the same longer-term trajectory. That was the message from Bill Dudley, New York Fed president last week, when he described the national economic effects of epic storms as "modest and transitory". Stormrelated turmoil could make it hard to read the economic data given the volatility it will insert into numbers, he said.

Michael Feroli of JPMorgan Chase does not expect Fed policy to be heavily affected. At next week's meeting it is still likely to announce the start to the process of reducing its balance sheet; it has not been making this move contingent on near-term swings in economic data.

"The next realistic chance to adjust the funds rate is not until mid-December, and at that point we think some of the more noticeable disruptions from the data flow should be behind us," added Mr Feroli.

None of this is to play down the short-term impact from two such violent storms. Harvey has triggered a surge in claims for unemployment benefits in Texas, for instance. Initial claims rose by 62,000 to a seasonally adjusted 298,000 in the week ended September 2, the biggest weekly jump since 2012, according to the Labor Department. In 2005 there were more than 500,000 hurricane-related jobless claims over three months following Katrina, according to High Frequency Economics.

Industrial production is likely to be dented by shutdowns in Texas's chemicals and petroleum sector. Power outages have affected more than half the homes and businesses in Florida, hitting utilities' output and business activity. The state's tourism sector has been particularly exposed to the storm's effects.

In addition, it may take time for rebuilding to get under way - especially in two states where there are shortages of construction labour. This could mean wages in the sector are boosted.

Goldman predicts the third-quarter hit from the two storms will be more than recouped over the subsequent three quarters as business investment and construction activity rallies. "You get an initial negative impact that is made up in subsequent months and quarters, and it washes out very quickly," said Mark Zandi of Moody's Analytics. "It should not have any material impact on monetary policy."

That does not necessarily mean the job of interpreting the coming weeks' data will be easy for the Fed. Inflation readings will probably be hit by the storms, at a time when the central bank is sensitive to fluctuations in prices.

D isappointments on inflation readings have left the Fed's interest-rate decisions finely balanced, with many investors doubting the central bank will be able to lift rates by another quarter-point by the end of the year. Greater clarity will come next Wednesday, when Ms Yellen gives a press conference after the central bank's latest meeting.

Exponential Technology Promises Fundamental Changes; Life spans, career paths, retirement face upheaval as technology disrupts traditional assumptions

704 words
13 September 2017
19:51
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Ric Edelman

 " Founder and executive chairman of Edelman Financial Services, Fairfax, Va.  " Education: B.A., Honorary Ph.D. Rowan University  " Professional Designations: Board Certified in Mutual Funds, Certified Fund Specialist, Chartered Mutual Fund Counselor, Certified Retirement Counselor, Qualified Financial Planner, Registered Financial Consultant

Background: Mr. Edelman, a best-selling author and a television and radio personality, began his career as a journalist covering financial issues in the 1980s. When Mr. Edelman and his wife hired a financial adviser who prompted them to lie on their mortgage application to qualify for a home loan, they were shocked. After their surprise ebbed, they felt inspired. "We thought, 'If this guy can make a living as an adviser giving people harmful advice, could we make a living giving someone helpful advice?' " Mr. Edelman says. He and his wife launched their own firm in 1986.

His practice: Mr. Edelman's practice currently serves over 34,000 clients, providing financial-planning services to anyone with at least $5,000 to invest. Mr. Edelman specializes in educating clients about how technology will affect their financial future. He says that rapidly advancing exponential technologies such as robotics, 3D printing and artificial intelligence will significantly increase the human life span, alter career paths, and put an end to traditional retirement.

The biggest challenge facing the advising industry: Mr. Edelman says most advisers aren't prepared for this type of longevity that will come with advances in medicine, neuroscience and robotics. "The typical adviser is using outdated financial-planning software that assumes their clients will live to 90 or 95," says Mr. Edelman. He says that this gap could put clients in serious financial jeopardy as they outlive their savings.

On helping clients prepare for change: Mr. Edelman takes a two-part approach to assisting his clients succeed financially in a rapidly changing world. The first step is exposing clients to information about exponential technologies and how it will affect their lives. The second is to provide them with investing opportunities that align with technological growth. Mr. Edelman has played a part in building such investment vehicles. In 2014, he worked with Morningstar and Blackrock to develop iShares Exponential Technologies ETF.

Advisers as career counselors: Mr. Edelman says that technology will eliminate many jobs, making it necessary for workers to be retrained in new industries. He says advisers will need to incorporate career counseling and planning into their practices and help clients plan to save money for additional education.

On games of strategy and advising: In his spare time, Mr. Edelman enjoys playing games of strategy such as pool, hearts and chess. His work and the games he enjoys are similar, he says. "It's all the strategic anticipation of what will happen next."

His proudest professional achievement & "Having been named by Barron's 3 times as No. 1 independent financial adviser," he says.

Compiled by Lynn Shattuck

Market Talk: Daily Analysis Roundup; Read our compilation of concise, timely analysis by Wall Street Journal reporters. Goldman Sachs's Smart-Beta ETF Will Expand Price War; Equifax Continues Slide; Harvey More Costly than Irma, Says Moody's Analytics

English

GSAM's Smart-Beta ETF Will Expand Price War

A planned smart-beta ETF from Goldman Sachs Asset Management with a management fee of 0.09% well below the management fees of other smart-beta ETFs aside from those sponsored by Vanguard expands the ETF price war beyond plain-vanilla ETFs, says Stephen Tu, senior analyst at Moody's. That's credit-negative for traditional active-equity players entering the smart-beta realm, including Legg Mason (LM), Franklin Resources (BEN) and Janus Capital (JNS), he says. It's also credit-negative for the existing smart-beta managers, including Invesco (IVZ), BlackRock (BLK), State Street (STT) and WisdomTree Investments (WETF), who are likely to respond with price cuts on existing products, Tu says. Thus far, most smart-beta ETFs have been priced between 24 basis points and 39 basis points, he says. BLK, STT and WETF didn't have an immediate comment. IVZ didn't immediately return a call seeking comment. (Daisy.Maxey@wsj.com; @DaisyMaxey)

Equifax Continues Slide As Data-Breach Complaints Mount

Shares in Equifax (EFX) are down 12% to $101.71 as the credit-reporting company continues to get scrutiny from customers, competitors, investors and lawmakers over the massive data breach it revealed last week. In an op-ed published in USA Today, CEO Richard Smith apologized for the breach impacting 143 million US consumers and added it has been the "most humbling moment" in its history. Meanwhile, Reuters reported that Sen Heidi Heitkamp (D, ND) has called for federal agencies to investigate whether top executives sold shares based on knowledge about the hack prior to Thursday's public announcement. The company has denied any wrongdoing. EFX had gained 21% this year prior to Friday, but shares are now down more than 13% this year, shaving off about $4.8 billion in value from the company. (bowdeya.tweh@wsj.com; @BowKnowsBiz)

Harvey More Costly than Irma, Says Moody's Analytics

Hurricane Harvey, which caused widespread flooding in the Houston area last month, was more costly to the US economy than Hurricane Irma, which made landfall in Florida on Sunday, Moody's Analytics says. Harvey cost the economy between $86 billion and $108 billion, with median estimate of $97 billion, Moody's Analytics says. Irma cost between $58 billion and $83 billion, with a median estimate of $70.5 billion. Harvey was seen as causing significantly more damage to residential and commercial property, making it a more costly storm, the analysis showed. However, Irma caused slightly more loss of economic output. A larger number of people evacuated ahead of Irma and still millions in the Southeast are without power. (eric.morath@wsj.com; @ericmorath)

Goldman Raises Deposit Limit

Goldman Sachs (GS) dropped the velvet rope in welcoming small-time savers last year, opening online savings accounts with as little as $1. Now it's welcoming the big money back in. GS has raised the maximum account size from $250,000 to $1 million for its online accounts, which pay 1.2% a year, one of the best rates out there. Growing deposits has been a key goal, especially as GS looks to lower its overall cost of funding a boon to its traders and ramp up its lending activity. GS has $15 billion in retail deposits, according to an investor presentation this week. (liz.hoffman@wsj.com; @lizrhoffman)

New Irma Damage Estimate: $25B

Hurricane Irma caused $18 billion in insured losses in the US and $7 billion in the Caribbean, according to the latest estimates from catastrophe modeling firm Karen Clark & Co. The estimates do not include crop losses or damage that is insured by the National Flood Insurance Program. In the Caribbean, half the property damage was in St. Martin, Barbuda, Anguilla and the British Virgin Islands, the company says. In the U.S., most of the devastation was in the Florida Keys, but Jacksonville and Charleston also experienced "significant inland flooding." (nicole.friedman@wsj.com; @NicoleFriedman)

Tax Reform Hopes Vanish from Bank Stocks

Bank stocks have recently underperformed assets that the group tends to be tied to, evidence that "investors are pricing in a very low probability of significant regulatory or tax reform," Goldman Sachs analysts write. Bank stocks tend to be linked to assets like interest rates, fixed income, the dollar, and the stock market. But after the election, bank stocks outperformed these assets by 12% as traders forecast a greater chance of policy changes, says Goldman Sachs, adding that the entire financial sector has been volatile since the election. The firm benchmarked returns of the KBW Nasdaq Bank Index with funds tracking the S&P 500, Treasurys, high yield bonds, gold and oil. "The Trump premium has drained out of banks," the analysts write. (gunjan.banerji@wsj.com; @gunjanjs)

SBI Life IPO to Raise Over $1 Billion

SBI Life Insurance, one of India's top life insurers, will raise as much as INR84 billion ($1.3 billion) in its initial share sale offer next week. This will be the largest IPO in the past seven years, after Coal India. The price band for the offer is fixed between INR 685-INR 700 a share and it will open on September 20. SBI Life is the second life insurer to be listed on the stock exchange. Last year, the country's largest private life insurer ICICI Prudential Life Insurance was listed. Many life insurance firms are coming out to list their shares to take advantage of a buoyant stock market and also due to regulatory requirements. (debiprasad.nayak@wsj.com)

San Mateo, Calif.-based Apttus Corp., which makes software that automates corporate transactions, has raised a $55 million Series E round.

Apttus, which is a member of The Wall Street Journal's Billion Dollar Startup Club, previously raised $88 million in September 2016, which brought the company's valuation to $1.3 billion. The latest funding brings the company's total funding to date to around $329 million.

CMO Today: Cord Cutting Ad Impact; Google's Olive Branch to Publishers; Media Titans at Goldman; Here's your morning roundup of the biggest marketing, advertising and media industry news and happenings.

By Alexandra Bruell 
1,226 words
13 September 2017
13:09
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Good morning. I'm filling in for Lara today and will do my best with the music puns. It was a big day for media and technology executives out on the scene. Apple held its highly anticipated product launch event marking the tenth anniversary of the original iPhone. The buzziest announcement "https://www.wsj.com/articles/apple-unveils-new-products-at-iphone-event-1505238436", which will cost $999, and come loaded with facial-recognition and augmented reality features. Also noteworthy was an animated poo emoji, as Reuters reporter Stephen Nellis "https://twitter.com/StephenNellis/status/907675971859263489". Meanwhile, buttoned-up executives from top media, marketing and technology firms made their case for change at the kickoff of Goldman Sachs' Communacopia conference, and the advertising technology conference DMexco begins Wednesday.

Whole Lotta Cord Cutting

On Monday, The Wall Street Journal reported that two media agencies downgraded their forecasts for global ad spending. Now eMarketer has "https://www.emarketer.com/Article/eMarketer-Lowers-US-TV-Ad-Spend-Estimate-Cord-Cutting-Accelerates/1016463"for U.S. TV ad spending due to "faster-than-expected growth in cord-cutting" and the corresponding audience shrinkage. The research firm predicts there will be over 22 million cord-cutters this year, up 33% over last year and a big step-up from its earlier estimate of 15.4 million. Consumer attention is shifting to new "over the top" streaming services. The average time spent watching TV (excluding digital) among U.S. adults will drop 3.1% to 3 hours 58 minutes this year, while consumption of digital video will increase 9.3% to 1 hour 17 minutes, according to the report. As a result, this year, eMarketer predicts U.S. TV ad spending will increase 0.5% to $71.65 billion -- down from the $72.72 billion it estimated in its first quarter forecast. TV's share of total media ad spending in the U.S. will drop from 36.6% last year to 34.9% this year, and will go below 30% by 2021.

You've Gotta Give For What You Take

Google's "first click free" program has become a headache for some subscription-based news publishers, including The Wall Street Journal. It lets people access stories via search results even if they aren't subscribers, and publishers who don't participate can find their sites penalized in the search rankings. Now "https://www.wsj.com/articles/google-offers-olive-branch-to-publishers-by-relaxing-policy-on-subscription-sites-1505259211", report WSJ's Jack Nicas and Lukas Alpert. Robert Thomson, CEO of WSJ-parent News Corp, let out the news at the Goldman Sachs media conference Tuesday. Subscription-oriented publishers will now be able to take whatever approach they want -- you can give away sample stories if you so desire, but if you opt to keep a strict paywall (zero free rides), as the Journal has done lately, you won't be penalized in the search results. That's due to an algorithm tweak that allows Google to scan entire news stories, not just the portions that are beyond the paywall. This represents a significant rapprochement after Google and publishers have sparred over a variety of issues. We'll see if the good vibes last.

I Like Big Data and I Cannot Lie

Elsewhere at the Goldman conference, the "it's all about data" theme kept surfacing. AT&T CEO Randall Stephenson used his time to tout the benefits of targeted advertising. AT&T is "building an advertising capability" for more precise ad targeting, he said. He said he expects the company's acquisition of Time Warner to "https://www.wsj.com/articles/at-t-to-give-free-hbo-to-more-wireless-customers-1505226248" from advertising, and added that AT&T could use data on viewing habits to select what shows and movies to invest in, reports WSJ. Later in the morning, Comcast CEO Brian Roberts urged marketers to use the company's analytics to inform their ad-buying, rather than relying only on Nielsen data, WSJ's Imani Moise reports. And he talked down the value of targeted ads on Facebook. On the content front, Netflix CFO David Wells said the company would pay $20 million an hour for a show if the circumstances were right (wow), WSJ's Joe Flint reports. And at a time when Amazon and Facebook are angling into live sports, Mr. Wells said Netflix has no plans to.

'Duopoly' Blues

Winning the eyebrow-raising award at the Goldman conference was WPP CEO Martin Sorrell, who referenced former White House chief strategist Steve Bannon to make a point about digital media giants Google and Facebook. He said: "I have no great truck for Steve Bannon, but before Bannon left for the White House he did say, 'Google and Facebook are utilities.' And he implied that utilities should be regulated." He appeared to be referring to remarks "https://theintercept.com/2017/07/27/steve-bannon-wants-facebook-and-google-regulated-like-utilities/". We're not sure if Mr. Sorrell was making the case for heightened regulation of Google and Facebook, or just opening it up for debate. What is clear is that no matter how Mr. Sorrell feels about the digital giants he refers to as "frenemies," WPP will continue to increase its spending with the companies. In his speech, Mr. Sorrell said that WPP's spending with Google will increase to $5.5 billion in 2017, from $5 billion last year, and spending with Facebook will get a boost from $1.7 billion to around $2.5 billion. In other WPP news, Mr. Sorrell said the company will be changing its regional management approach and encouraging more integration on shared client work across agencies.

Banking & Finance: Banks Weigh Shift From Equifax --- TransUnion, Experian are poised to pick up business in the wake of big data  breach

By AnnaMaria Andriotis and Emily Glazer 
514 words
13 September 2017
The Wall Street Journal
J
B14
English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Banks and other financial companies are considering the possibility of moving some business away from Equifax Inc. in the wake of its data breach and to some of the firm's credit-reporting rivals, according to people familiar with the firms.

Lenders are unlikely to take any immediate action and are seeking more information from Equifax about the hack that exposed sensitive personal financial information about potentially 143 million Americans. Still, large banks, in particular, have expressed dismay privately that their customers' information was compromised, that they received no advance warning of the breach announcement, and that they still have little insight into what went wrong, these people said.

Equifax didn't immediately respond to requests for comment.

Equifax has already faced widespread criticism from consumers. In the aftermath of the breach disclosure many consumers said they struggled with the firm's website and policies.

Equifax is also facing political ire: Two congressional committees are planning hearings into the hack, and the Senate Finance Committee on Monday sent the company a list of requests for information about the attack, the company's response to it, and who knew what and when about the problem.

Executives at financial firms in recent days have publicly expressed concern about the breach. James Dimon, chief executive officer of J.P. Morgan Chase & Co., the biggest U.S. bank by assets, spoke about Equifax twice at conferences on Tuesday.

Mr. Dimon said he is interested in understanding more about whether the hack is the sort of attack that could have happened to any company or if there was something specific that Equifax didn't do correctly. "All that will matter, it's obviously important," he said.

He also said, "It depends what happened and how it happened, whether they could've or should've." Mr. Dimon said that the bank was in contact with Equifax.

Speaking at a conference the day before, Capital One Financial Corp. CEO Richard Fairbank said the cost of the attack would be borne by both consumers and financial companies. "A bunch of our customers are affected," he said. "It's going to be costly to them and to us."

It is unlikely that financial firms would cease doing business with Equifax altogether. But, as contracts with the company come up for renewal, they might look to shift some of their business to rivals such as TransUnion or Experian PLC, the people familiar with the matter said.

For most loans, excluding mortgages, lenders often don't check credit reports from all three big credit-reporting firms. Moving away from Equifax would likely result in more market share to TransUnion and Experian.

The possibility of losing market share could add to the pressure on Equifax, which is already facing lawsuits and investigations related to the breach. The company's stock has fallen nearly 20% since just before the hack was announced last week.

Pro Cyber
Open-Source Software in Spotlight After Equifax Breach

By Ben DPietro 
396 words
12 September 2017
12:33
WSJ Pro Cybersecurity
RSTPROCY
English
Copyright  2017, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

As cybersleuths work to uncover the exact vulnerability hackers exploited to pull off the Equifax Inc. data theft, one thing companies not wanting to be the next Equifax can do is review the types of open-source software used in applications they deploy and then look for ways to more effectively mitigate those threats.

A recent report from Sonatype--a software supply chain automation firm--found just 57% of organizations have a software governance policy, while 65% don't have meaningful controls over what components go into the apps they use.

Some software specialists say there is little hope for an organization to undertake such a review without the use of technology. "Even for organizations that are aware and concerned, if they are dealing with this problem through any mechanism that isn't automated, they are going to have a hard time scaling," said Wayne Jackson, chief executive of Sonatype.

Having an automated process can help catch errors that manual systems usually miss, said Mr. Jackson, citing the potential for misnaming deliberately or not.

"If you're a relatively sophisticated organization you need to create an organizational inventory of where open-source exists in your software infrastructure &then prioritize remediation based on risk," said Mr. Jackson. "If I'm Bank XYZ, I want to know what my apps are, what's in them and if what is on them is good or bad."

Lev Lesokhin, executive vice president of strategy and analytics at CAST, a software analysis and measurement company, said executives involved in cybersecurity should take an inventory of the open-source components used in their business-critical software and identify the specific vulnerabilities to which they might be particularly susceptible.

"Organizations that use a lot of open-source components should put tighter structural software-quality controls in place for those applications which control critical data," he said.

"Teams should make sure they are using the latest automation to control the way their open-source frameworks are configured and how they interface to your own custom components."

(Ben DiPietro is an editor and reporter for Risk & Compliance Journal. He worked previously as online editor at seafood industry news service Intrafish and as a reporter with Associated Press and Pacific Business News in Hawaii. Write to Ben at ben.dipietro@wsj.com)

Markets
Equifax Hack Could Slow Down Fast Loans; In the wake of the breach, online lenders re-evaluate quick approval procedures for confirming a potential borrower's identity

By Telis Demos 
1,066 words
11 September 2017
22:01
WSJ Pro Venture Capital
RSTPROVC
English
Copyright  2017, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

The goal of digital lending in recent years has been to make credit decisions quickly and cheaply. The Equifax Inc. data breach could force some lenders to hit the brakes.

Lenders and data providers said that in the wake of the breach "https://www.wsj.com/articles/equifax-reports-data-breach-possibly-impacting-143-million-u-s-consumers-1504819765", they are evaluating procedures for confirming a potential borrower's identity. Equifax, like other credit-reporting companies, played a big role in helping these firms verify borrowers' identities, selling them access to a database that could quickly match up a person's identifying information.

Unlike traditional banks, which may lend to people who walk into a branch, or to customers they know well through checking accounts or other products, newer online lenders take applications from unknown people, entirely online.

To compete with banks, firms such as LendingClub Corp. and Kabbage Inc. often promise near-instant approval for online personal or small-business loans, and make it easy to submit applications online or even through a mobile app. Some firms, like Quicken Loans Inc.'s Rocket Mortgage, even offer the same for home loans.

To offer such speedy access, one measure used by many lenders is to check customers' submitted Social Security numbers and other personal information against databases owned by Equifax and peers TransUnion and Experian PLC. If the data match up, the person is likely who they say they are.

Such a check is relatively inexpensive, roughly 5 cents to 50 cents per application, according to lenders. The price is low because such databases are well established and don't involve sophisticated analysis, and are effective enough in catching more blatant fraud attempts.

Now, however, those checks could become less effective in weeding out someone putting in a loan application with a false identity. The Equifax breach compromised personal identification data "https://www.wsj.com/articles/equifax-customer-complaints-continue-to-pile-up-1505080789" exposing names, dates of birth, addresses and Social Security numbers. In some cases, drivers' license data may have also been exposed.

"Most banks and many financial technology companies rely on drivers' license validation or Social Security validation," said Zach Perret, chief executive of Plaid Technologies Inc., which connects companies to bank data for transacting and verification. "It will be hard to rely on them to verify identities going forward."

The complexity for these lenders is that updating procedures can be difficult, expensive and time-consuming. Tools such as verifying images of a driver's license or using a phone's thumbprint reader can cost as much as $10 per application, lenders say. Firms also have added steps such as making small deposits in a borrower's bank account, and verifying them, before making a loan.

"There are some really great tools out there, but the industry today mostly uses older [identification] methodologies," Mr. Perret said. "Even though we know that 143 million peoples' data has been compromised, it could still take years for the industry to catch up and move to new identity validation standards."

LendingClub and Kabbage said they have previously incorporated tools beyond credit bureaus into their verification methods and were monitoring fraud rates for any sign of uptick. Quicken Loans declined to comment.

Dave Girouard, CEO of online lender Upstart Network Inc., said that as a rule of thumb, each additional document a company asks a borrower to produce reduces completed applications by 15%.

Upstart does verify borrowers' income information, which it says weeds out more bad loans, and is on balance worth the cost. It also asks for information such as where a person went to school and verifies bank accounts.

These tools can be relatively expensive, and some customers may not want to give up that information. But Mr. Girouard said the extra steps have curtailed fraud.

Lenders' profit margin from making a loan, which then tends to be sold on to investors, tends to be thin to begin with. And for every loan they make, lenders turn down hundreds or thousands of applicants. Identify verification, which helps to reduce fraud and fulfills regulatory obligations to know who you are lending to, is a big part of the cost of lending.

Plus, asking for more information perhaps the easiest way to update security procedures can discourage some legitimate borrowers.

Scott Carter, CEO of ID Analytics LLC, said that while larger lenders already tend to use more advanced methods for verifying identity, many firms may now need to invest in new systems.

"Smaller lenders with less volume can be less sophisticated in their fraud practices and this can impact their exposure to new fraud threats," he said. "But there are exceptions. There are a few small lenders that are very sophisticated and a few large ones that aren't."

Some lenders said they breathed a sigh of relief that the Equifax breach didn't extend to deeper data about customers, such as their detailed history of things like prior addresses or credit amounts outstanding. They also said that past breaches have already made them wary of basic identifying information.

One executive of a company that works with several lenders on verification, including some that use Equifax, said theft of detailed credit histories specific details of a person's borrowings and credit cards, for example would have been far worse than what occurred, the "nuclear weapon" of credit fraud. In that case, even lenders using more advanced steps, like testing whether a borrower can pick out an address where they have never lived, would be compromised.

"When I first saw [the Equifax headline], my heart sank," he said. "There would be no way to tell when identity theft occurs."

Still, because the stolen data included Social Security numbers, driver's licenses and other identifying information, lenders said the breach was more dangerous than, say, thefts of credit-card numbers. Those can be changed and aren't used to confirm borrower identity.

So even firms that aren't Equifax clients are weighing whether they need to make changes.

"We immediately started looking at alternatives, to ensure that we're ready to handle what may come right now," said Krishna Gopinathan, CEO of Applied Data Finance, an online lending credit-analysis firm that doesn't use Equifax for identity verification.

Equifax Complaints Pile Up | Reinsurers to Largely Pay for Irma Damage | Campaign-Finance Riders | Hayashi's Take: What Hack Means for CFPB; The Wall Street Journal's financial regulation newsletter for Monday, September 11, 2017.
